import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForSequenceClassification, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForSequenceClassification.from_pretrained(
            self.model_name, config=self.config, trust_remote_code=True
        )

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "LongSafari/hyenadna-small-32k-seqlen-hf"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name, trust_remote_code=True)
config.num_labels = 2
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 32000
batch_size = 1

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

For HyenaDNA with 32k length, one key observation is that the sequence dimension is very large. This means that parallelizing over the sequence dimension in custom kernels would be very efficient. A kernel that does element-wise operations across multiple tensors along the sequence dimension would use all SMs effectively. For example, consider the gating operation in Hyena. We have `order+1` branches, each of shape (batch, length, dim). We need to multiply them all together element-wise, then apply an output projection. A naive PyTorch implementation would: 1. Multiply branch 0 and branch 1: read 2 tensors, write 1 (3 * B * L * D memory) 2. Multiply result with branch 2: read 2 tensors, write 1 (3 * B * L * D memory) 3. Apply output projection: read B * L * D, write B * L * D (2 * B * L * D memory + compute) Total for just these ops: ~8 * B * L * D memory traffic, plus GEMM. A fused kernel could: 1. For each position, load all branches, multiply them, apply output projection 2. It typically has: - `in_proj`: Linear(d_model, order * d_model) or similar - `out_proj`: Linear(d_model, d_model) - `short_filter`: Short convolution - Some mechanism for long convolutions If the Hyena operator is parameterized with implicit long filters, the forward might be: ```python class HyenaOperator(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): self.in_proj = nn.Linear(d_model, (order+1) * d_model) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(d_model, d_model) self.short_filter = nn.Conv1d(d_model, d_model, kernel_size, groups=d_model) self.filter_fn = HyenaFilter(...) def forward(self, u): # u: (B, L, D) uc = self.short_filter(u.transpose(1,2)).transpose(1,2) # short conv x = self.in_proj(uc) # project to branches # split into order+1 branches # long convolutions via implicit filters + FFT # multiply branches together # output projection ``` Actually, the typical Hyena operator uses: - A short convolution on the input - Then projects to order+1 branches - The first branch goes through a short convolution - The remaining branches go through FFT-based long convolutions with implicitly parameterized filters - Then branches are multiplied together (gating) - Output projection For implementation details, the `hyenadna` code on HuggingFace defines these classes. Let me think about what specifically might be slow in the reference. One common issue with `trust_remote_code=True` models is that they may not use the most efficient PyTorch operations. For example: - Using `einsum` for convolutions instead of `nn.Conv1d` - Computing filters on-the-fly in Python loops - Not using optimal data formats - Using `torch.fft.fft` and `torch.fft.ifft` without optimal normalization For the Hyena operator, the typical forward in these models is: ```python def forward(self, u): # u shape: (batch, length, dim) l = u.len() u = u.transpose(1, 2) # (B, D, L) # short convolution uc = self.short_filter(u) # depthwise conv1d uc = uc[..., :l] # project to order+1 branches x = self.in_proj(uc.transpose(1, 2)) # (B, L, (order+1)*D) *x, v = x.chunk(order+1, dim=-1) # x[0], x[1], ..., v # for each branch, apply long conv via FFT for i, xi in enumerate(x): # implicit filter k = self.filter_fn(l, dim=i) # frequency response # FFT, multiply, IFFT xi_f = torch.fft.rfft(xi, dim=1) k_f = k.view(1, -1, k.shape[-1]) out_f = xi_f * k_f x[i] = torch.fft.irfft(out_f, n=l, dim=1) # gating: multiply all branches and v y = v for xi in x: y = y * xi # output projection y = self.out_proj(y) return y ``` If the model does this, then per layer we have: 1. Short conv: 1 conv1d 2. In projection: 1 Linear 3. For each branch: 1 FFT, 1 complex multiply, 1 IFFT 4. Gating: order elementwise multiplies 5. Out projection: 1 Linear For order=2, that's 2 branches + v = 3 chunks. - 2 FFT-IFFT pairs for long conv - 2 element multiplies for gating (v * x0, then * x1) - Plus short conv, in_proj, out_proj If I could write a kernel that fuses the gating multiplies and output projection, that would save memory bandwidth. And more importantly, if I could fuse multiple FFT-IFFT operations together... But we can't write FFT in a custom kernel (well, we can call cuFFT). The pointwise complex multiplies COULD be fused with the FFTs if we had control over the FFT implementation. But since we don't, the best we can do is fuse the element-wise operations. Actually, wait. Could I write a custom kernel that calls cuFFT? In theory, yes. In a C++ extension, I can include `<cufft.h>` and use `cufftExecR2C`, `cufftExecC2R`. But integrating this with PyTorch's tensor lifecycle is complex. PyTorch's `torch.fft.rfft` and `torch.fft.irfft` should already be calling cuFFT optimally. The only way to improve them would be to: 1. Fuse surrounding operations 2. Use a better FFT plan (but PyTorch already does planning) 3. What if the reference implementation uses the Python-loop approach for the Hyena branches (computing each branch's FFT in a for loop)? Python for loops in model forward are SLOW. If the model does something like: ```python for i in range(order): branch = branches[i] # do FFT # ... ``` Then for each branch, it launches FFT kernels separately. This could add significant CPU overhead and prevent kernel overlap. If instead we batch the FFTs across branches (e.g., by stacking them as an extra batch dimension), we could launch fewer, larger FFTs. This would save kernel launch overhead and potentially allow better FFT utilization. A custom kernel or custom Python code could: - Stack all branches into a single tensor - Launch a single batched FFT - Do all pointwise multiplies - Launch a single batched IFFT This is a pure Python optimization but could be very impactful if the original model uses Python loops. Similarly, if the model processes the filter MLP in a Python loop over positions or dimensions, we could vectorize it. These are actually very likely to be impactful because many research codebases (which `trust_remote_code` models often are) don't optimize for inference. So maybe my strategy should be: 1. If batched as [1, 2, 32000, 256] or [2, 32000, 256], cuFFT can process all at once. cuFFT should be efficient in either case, but there might be kernel launch overhead for separate calls. From what I know about HyenaDNA on HuggingFace, the code looks something like: ```python class HyenaDNAForSequenceClassification(PreTrainedModel): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__(config) self.num_labels = config.num_labels self.model = HyenaDNAModel(config) self.score = nn.Linear(config.d_model, config.num_labels) def forward(self, input_ids, ...): hidden_states = self.model(input_ids) pooled = hidden_states[:, -1] # or mean pool logits = self.score(pooled) return SequenceClassifierOutput(logits=logits) class HyenaDNAModel(PreTrainedModel): def __init__(self, config): self.embeddings = HyenaDNAEmbeddings(config) self.layers = nn.ModuleList([HyenaBlock(config) for _ in range(config.n_layer)]) def forward(self, input_ids): hidden_states = self.embeddings(input_ids) for layer in self.layers: hidden_states = layer(hidden_states) return hidden_states class HyenaBlock(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): self.norm1 = nn.LayerNorm(config.d_model) self.mixer = HyenaOperator(...) self.norm2 = nn.LayerNorm(config.d_model) self.mlp = nn.Sequential( nn.Linear(config.d_model, config.d_inner), nn.GELU(), nn.Linear(config.d_inner, config.d_model), nn.Dropout(config.dropout), ) def forward(self, hidden_states): hidden_states = hidden_states + self.mixer(self.norm1(hidden_states)) hidden_states = hidden_states + self.mlp(self.norm2(hidden_states)) return hidden_states ``` For the `HyenaOperator`, it's more complex. The typical operator: 1. Takes (B, L, D) input 2. `self.short_filter` applies a depthwise conv 3. `self.in_proj` projects to (order+1)*D 4. Splits into order+1 chunks 5. Applies long convolutions via implicit filters to some branches 6. Gating: multiplies all branches 7. Output projection For the filters, there might be a `HyenaFilter` that computes frequency responses. If I can write a custom module that replaces `HyenaBlock` and implements the forward with custom kernels for the MLP and maybe some parts of the operator, that would be ideal. But the exact structure of `HyenaOperator` depends on the specific implementation. Wait, what if I just replace the `mlp` Sequential in each block with a custom module that uses the fused kernel? That's a targeted, safe optimization. Let me write a custom `FusedMLP` module: ```python class FusedMLP(nn.Module): def __init__(self, layer_norm, linear1, activation, linear2, dropout): super().__init__() self.norm = layer_norm self.w1 = linear1.weight self.b1 = linear1.bias self.w2 = linear2.weight self.b2 = linear2.bias def forward(self, x, residual): return fused_mlp_kernel(x, residual, self.norm.weight, self.norm.bias, self.w1, self.b1, self.w2, self.b2) ``` And then in `ModelNew.__init__`, I replace the MLP in each block: ```python for block in self.model.model.layers: mlp = block.mlp # mlp is typically [Linear, GELU, Linear, Dropout] # Replace with FusedMLP ``` But I need to know the exact structure of `block.mlp`. Alternatively, I can replace the BLOCK's forward entirely: ```python class CustomBlock(nn.Module): def __init__(self, block): super().__init__() self.block = block self.fused_mlp = FusedMLP(block.norm2, block.mlp[0], block.mlp[1], block.mlp[2]) def forward(self, hidden_states): hidden_states = hidden_states + self.block.mixer(self.block.norm1(hidden_states)) hidden_states = self.fused_mlp(hidden_states, hidden_states) return hidden_states ``` Hmm, but I can't easily replace the forward of a module instance without monkey-patching. Actually I CAN: ```python block.forward = lambda self, hs: custom_forward(hs) ``` Or: ```python class NewBlock(nn.Module): def __init__(self, block): super().__init__() self.norm1 = block.norm1 self.mixer = block.mixer self.norm2 = block.norm2 self.mlp = block.mlp def forward(self, hidden_states): hidden_states = hidden_states + self.mixer(self.norm1(hidden_states)) # Use custom fused kernel for MLP residual = hidden_states normed = self.norm2(hidden_states) mlp_out = self.mlp(normed) hidden_states = residual + mlp_out return hidden_states ``` Actually, I could just write the forward WITHOUT replacing the MLP, but add `torch.jit.script` or `torch.compile`? The groups parameter might cause overhead For depthwise conv with kernel=3, a custom kernel could process each channel independently and be very efficient. Actually, in the evaluation, the timing is likely done after a warm-up run. So first-forward effects don't matter. Alright, let me think about what custom kernel I should write. For maximum impact on H100, I want to: 1. Minimize memory bandwidth 2. Use Tensor Cores when possible (for GEMMs) - but we can't write better GEMM kernels than cuBLAS 3. Minimize kernel launch overhead Since writing a custom GEMM kernel is very hard and unlikely to beat cuBLAS, and cuBLAS already uses Tensor Cores, the main opportunity is memory bandwidth reduction through fusion. I'll write a fused kernel for the standard transformer block pattern: `residual + mlp(norm(input))`. But wait, for the MLP part, I can use a standard transformer-like kernel pattern. Actually, there's another pattern: in some implementations, the MLP uses Gated Linear Units (GLU), where there are TWO linear projections that are element-wise multiplied, then a third linear. But HyenaDNA might use standard GeLU MLP. Actually, from the paper and code, HyenaDNA uses a standard MLP: ```python x = x + W2 @ gelu(W1 @ norm(x)) ``` For my fused kernel, I'll handle: Input: x (the input to the layer, also the residual) Output: x + MLP(LayerNorm(x)) The kernel will: 1. For each token position, load x from memory 2. Compute LayerNorm(x) using shared memory for statistics 3. Compute `gelu(LN(x) @ W1 + b1)` - but this involves a matmul. For d_model=256 and expand=1024, this is a [256] @ [256, 1024] = [1024] matmul per token. 4. Compute `y @ W2 + b2` where y is [1024] and W2 is [1024, 256] 5. Add residual 6. Write result Steps 3 and 4 are GEMMs. Doing them per token in a custom kernel is tricky because: - W1 and W2 are large matrices (256x1024 and 1024x256) - For each token, we do 2 small GEMMs - Parallelizing across tokens, each block can use shared memory for W1/W2 and cooperative GEMM This is actually feasible! For 32000 tokens, we can have: - Each thread block handles a group of tokens (e.g., 64 tokens) - Load W1 into shared memory (256 * 1024 * 4 = 1MB) - For each token, load its LN(x) vector, compute matmul with W1 using shared memory W1 - Similarly for W2 Wait, 1MB of shared memory is fine on H100 (up to 227KB per block though...). Hmm, 256 * 1024 * 4 = 1MB is too large for shared memory per block. We need to tile the GEMM. For W1 = [256, 1024], we can load tiles of W1 (e.g., 256 x 64) into shared memory, compute partial results, accumulate. Actually, for a per-token matmul: - `LN(x)` is a 256-element vector - `W1` is 256 x 1024 - `y = LN(x) @ W1` is a 1024-element vector This is essentially a vector-matrix multiplication. We can parallelize across the 1024 output dimension. - Load `LN(x)` vector into shared memory (256 floats = 1KB) - Each thread computes a few output elements by doing dot products with columns of W1 - For `y @ W2`, similar approach This is actually very efficient because: 1. `LN(x)` is shared across all threads in the block (read once from global memory) 2. We can load tiles of W1 and W2 into shared memory for reuse 3. The residual load and final write are coalesced For 32000 tokens, we can have a grid of (32000/batch_per_block, 1, 1) where each block handles, say, 1 token and 1024 threads compute the matmuls. Actually, 1024 threads per block is good. Each block handles 1 token: - 256 threads for the first matmul (each handles 4 outputs of size 1024/256=4) - Load LN(x) (256 floats) into shared mem - Load a tile of W1 (256 x 4 floats) into shared mem? No, W1 needs to be read from global. - Actually, for `vector @ matrix`, each thread reads the vector and 4 columns of W1. Wait, this is getting complicated. Let me think about a simpler approach. For the first matmul: `LN(x) [256] @ W1 [256, 1024] -> y [1024]` - We're essentially computing `y[j] = sum_i LN(x)[i] * W1[i, j]` - For 1024 threads per block, each thread computes 1 element of y. - All threads read `LN(x)` cooperatively into shared memory (256 floats ~ 1KB) - Thread j reads the j-th column of W1 (256 floats) and computes the dot product. - But reading 256 floats per thread from global memory is a lot. For 1024 threads, if each reads a column of 256 floats, that's 1024 * 256 = 262K floats = 1MB of global memory reads per block. For 32000 blocks, that's 32GB of memory reads just for W1. But W1 is read many times! This would be terrible. Actually, W1 is cached in L2, so subsequent reads are fast. But the total volume is still high. Alternatively, we can tile across both the token dimension and the output dimension: - Grid: (num_tokens / tokens_per_block, num_outputs / outputs_per_thread) - For each block, load a tile of tokens and a tile of W1 into shared memory Actually, for `vector @ matrix`, the optimal GPU implementation is to have: - Threads organized as a 2D grid over (tokens, outputs) - For token t and output j, compute `dot(LN(x[t]), W1[:, j])` - This is a batched vector-matrix product cuBLAS handles this efficiently via the GEMM: `[32000, 256] @ [256, 1024] -> [32000, 1024]`. In our custom kernel, if we want to fuse everything, we need to embed the GEMM logic. But for a matrix of size [32000, 256] @ [256, 1024], cuBLAS will be much more efficient than any simple custom kernel because it uses Tensor Cores, sophisticated tiling, and warp specialization. So if we try to replace the cuBLAS GEMM with a naive custom implementation, we'll likely be SLOWER, not faster. The only way to win is to fuse the elementwise operations around the GEMM without slowing down the GEMM. But the GEMM must be done by cuBLAS to be fast. Wait, but can we call cuBLAS from inside our custom kernel? In a C++ custom op, we can call `at::matmul` or `at::addmm`, which will call cuBLAS. Then we do elementwise operations in the same kernel? No, because `at::matmul` launches its own CUDA kernels. So what we CAN do is: 1. In our custom C++ op, call `at::matmul` for the GEMMs 2. Between them, call custom kernels for the elementwise ops But that's just what PyTorch does. The only difference is we reduce Python overhead. Actually, for a sequence of operations: ``` y1 = ln(x) y2 = matmul(y1, W1) + b1 y3 = gelu(y2) y4 = matmul(y3, W2) + b2 output = x + y4 ``` This is 5 kernel launches in PyTorch: 1. native_layer_norm_kernel 2. addmm_kernel (or matmul -> bias_add) 3. gelu_kernel 4. addmm_kernel 5. add_kernel If we write a custom C++ op that does: ```cpp auto y1 = at::native_layer_norm(x, ...); auto y2 = at::addmm(b1, y1, W1); auto y3 = at::gelu(y2); auto y4 = at::addmm(b2, y3, W2); auto out = x + y4; return out; ``` This is STILL 5 kernel launches because each at:: op launches a separate kernel. The advantage is zero Python overhead between them, but Python overhead is tiny (~1-2 microseconds). So this won't help much. To actually fuse operations, we need to write a SINGLE kernel that does everything. Which means we can't use cuBLAS for the GEMMs inside that kernel. This is the core dilemma. For the memory bandwidth savings to matter, the non-GEMM ops must be a significant portion of the runtime. For the MLP: - LN: ~0.1ms (memory bound) - GEMM1: ~2ms - GELU: ~0.05ms - GEMM2: ~2ms - Add: ~0.02ms Total: ~4.2ms. Non-GEMM: ~0.17ms. If we fuse non-GEMM ops together, we save ~0.17ms per layer. For 4 layers: ~0.7ms. Not much compared to ~16ms total. The GEMMs dominate the runtime. And we can't fuse our own kernels with cuBLAS GEMMs. So our opportunity for speedup from kernel fusion is limited. Wait, what about the other operations in the model? The Hyena operator involves: - Short conv - In_proj (GEMM) - FFTs - Complex multiplies - IFFTs - Gating (elementwise) - Out_proj (GEMM) The FFTs and GEMMs dominate. The elementwise ops are a small fraction. So where can we get speedup? 1. FFT optimization: PyTorch's FFT should be near-optimal. 2. GEMM optimization: PyTorch's matmul should be near-optimal. 3. Kernel fusion: small gains for elementwise ops. 4. Avoiding overhead: Python loops, tensor allocations. If the reference model has significant overhead from: - Python for-loops over branches - Dynamic filter computation - Suboptimal tensor layouts - Redundant copies Then fixing these could yield big speedups. For instance, if the model does: ```python for branch in branches: x_fft = torch.fft.rfft(branch, dim=1) x_fft = x_fft * filter[i] x_ifft = torch.fft.irfft(x_fft, dim=1) ``` This creates temporary tensors in each iteration. In a custom implementation, we could pre-allocate and reuse FFT buffers. The actual compute time is the same, but we save allocation overhead and potentially memory traffic. If the model allocates ~200MB of temporary tensors per layer and has 4 layers, that's ~800MB of allocation overhead. On H100, allocation is quite fast, but it's still overhead. More importantly, if the model transposes tensors back and forth between (B,L,D) and (B,D,L) for convolutions, each transpose causes a memory copy or at least a metadata overhead. Let me look at how to detect and optimize these issues. One specific issue I've seen in some Hyena implementations: they compute the long convolution filters using explicit convolution in the time domain instead of FFT. For length 32000, a time-domain convolution with a 32000-length filter would be O(32000^2) = 1B ops per channel, which is INSANE. But Hyena uses FFT-based convolution specifically to avoid this. Another issue: the short conv filters might be applied via explicit `F.conv1d` with padding, which is fine. Wait, I just thought of something. What if the sequence length 32000 causes the FFT to use a non-power-of-2 size, which is slower than a padded power-of-2 FFT? For sequence length 32000, the FFT is on 32000 points. 32000 = 2^7 * 5^3 = 128 * 125. cuFFT can handle this reasonably well, but it's not as fast as 32768 = 2^15. If the model doesn't pad to 32768, but we can pad and then truncate, we might get a faster FFT. PyTorch's `torch.fft.rfft(x, n=N)` automatically pads to N. If the model passes `n=32000`, then the FFT size is exactly 32000. But we could potentially use `n=32768` for a faster FFT, then truncate the IFFT output back to 32000. The IFFT for a padded size would give the correct result (circular convolution modulo 32768 vs modulo 32000), but if we're doing linear convolution, we need to be careful. Actually, for the implicit long filter in Hyena, the filter is carefully designed to give a specific linear convolution. If we change the FFT size, we'd need to adjust the filter response accordingly. So we can't arbitrarily change the FFT size without modifying the filter computation. If the filter is computed to match the exact FFT size, we can't change it. But if the filter is given as a time-domain sequence of length 32000, we could zero-pad both the input and filter to 32768, compute FFT, multiply, IFFT, and truncate. The result would be a linear convolution of length (32000+32000-1). But if we only need the first 32000 outputs, and the operations are designed for circular convolution... this gets complicated. Without knowing the exact filter design, it's risky to change the FFT size. OK, another idea: what if I can use real-to-complex FFT instead of complex FFT? The CUDA kernel: ```c++ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void fused_mlp_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ residual, const float* __restrict__ norm_weight, const float* __restrict__ norm_bias, const float* __restrict__ w1, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2, const float* __restrict__ b2, float* __restrict__ out, int batch, int seq_len, int dim, int inner_dim ) { // Each block handles one token int idx = blockIdx.x; if (idx >= batch * seq_len) return; // Shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* s_normed = smem; // dim float* s_hidden = smem + dim; // inner_dim (could be large) // But inner_dim might be 1024, and dim might be 256 // dim + inner_dim = 1280 floats = ~5KB. That's fine per block. // But wait, we can't have inner_dim in shared mem easily if it's large. // We can compute GELU and store temporarily. // Actually let's compute layer norm first const float* x_ptr = x + idx * dim; float* out_ptr = out + idx * dim; // Compute mean and variance float mean = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < dim; i += blockDim.x) { mean += x_ptr[i]; } // Reduce mean __shared__ float s_mean; // ... reduction code ... // This is getting very complicated for a simple fused kernel. } ``` You know what, writing a full LayerNorm + GEMM + GELU + GEMM + Add kernel is very complex. What if the speedup is achievable by using a custom kernel that implements the `HyenaOperator` forward pass in a more efficient way? Specifically, we can: 1. FUSE the short convolution with the input projection 2. FUSE the branch multiplications with the output projection 3. Pre-allocate and reuse FFT buffers For #1: Short conv is depthwise conv1d. Input proj is Linear(BL, D, 3D). These are both linear-ish operations. Can we fuse them? The short conv output is (B, L, D). The in_proj is `output @ W_in_proj + b_in_proj`. We could fuse them into a custom kernel that: 1. For each token, computes the short conv using cached weight 2. Immediately applies the linear projection But depthwise conv is actually a reduction over the kernel dimension for each channel. For kernel=3: `out[b, l, d] = sum_k w[d, k] * in[b, l+k-1, d]` Then in_proj is `out[b, l, :] @ W + b`. Fusing these means each thread block loads a tile of input and conv weights, computes the conv, then does the matmul with W. This avoids writing the intermediate conv output to global memory. Savings: 32000 * 256 * 4 = 32.7MB per layer. At 3.35TB/s: ~0.01ms. Not much. For #2: Fusing branch multiplies with out_proj. The branches are (order+1) tensors of shape (B, L, D). We multiply them elementwise to get (B, L, D), then apply out_proj. A kernel could: 1. Load all branch vectors for a position 2. Multiply them 3. Multiply by W_out and add b_out 4. Write result If done per token, this saves writing the gated tensor. Savings: same as before, ~32.7MB per layer = ~0.01ms. Neither of these is huge. For #3: Pre-allocating FFT buffers. The model might allocate `torch.fft.rfft` output tensors dynamically. PyTorch's FFT implementation often creates new tensors. If we pre-allocate and reuse them with `torch.fft.rfft(out=preallocated)`, we save allocation overhead. But PyTorch's `torch.fft.rfft` doesn't support an `out` parameter! We can't easily reuse FFT outputs. In a custom C++ op, we could call cuFFT directly with pre-allocated buffers. That might help. Actually, PyTorch's `torch.fft.rfft` DOES support `out` parameter: ```python torch.fft.rfft(input, n=None, dim=-1, norm=None, out=None) ``` So we CAN preallocate and reuse! This is a great optimization. For each layer and each branch, instead of allocating new complex tensors for FFT outputs, we can reuse a pre-allocated buffer. For a model with 4 layers and 2 branches per layer, each FFT output is [1, 16001, 256] complex32 (8 bytes) = ~32.7MB. If we preallocate 2 buffers and reuse them: - We avoid allocating 8 * 32.7MB = 261MB per forward pass - Allocation overhead on H100: maybe 0.5-1ms total for 261MB Not huge, but measurable. And we also save the overhead of allocating the IFFT output buffers. Actually, for memory allocation, PyTorch uses a caching allocator. If the same sizes are allocated repeatedly, the allocator reuses cached blocks. So the allocation overhead might already be amortized. But there might still be some overhead from the allocator's bookkeeping and zero-filling. Hmm. Let me think about whether torch.fft with out= helps. Actually, looking at PyTorch docs: `torch.fft.rfft(input, dim=-1, norm=None, *, out=None)`. Yes, it does support out parameter. But the output must have the right shape and dtype. For [1, 32000, 256] real input, the RFFT output is [1, 16001, 256] complex64, which is torch.complex64. We'd need to preallocate: ```python fft_buffer = torch.empty(1, 16001, 256, dtype=torch.complex64, device='cuda') ``` Then in the forward: ```python torch.fft.rfft(branch, dim=1, out=fft_buffer) ``` This avoids allocation. For the IFFT, we'd need another pre-allocated real buffer: ```python ifft_buffer = torch.empty(1, 32000, 256, device='cuda') torch.fft.irfft(fft_result, n=32000, dim=1, out=ifft_buffer) ``` This could save some allocation overhead per layer. But the actual FFT computation still takes the same time. For a model that runs in ~20ms, saving 1ms from avoiding allocations is a 5% speedup. That's something, but probably not enough to "win." Actually, let me reconsider the custom kernel approach. What if I write a custom kernel that's NOT for the internal model, but for the ENTIRE problem? Like, a kernel that computes the model's forward pass by reading weights directly and doing all the math. For a model with ~5M parameters and d_model=256, the weights are: - Embeddings: 32000 * 256 = 8.2M (actually vocab_size is DNA bases, maybe 12) For vocab=12: 12 * 256 = 3K floats = 12KB - Per layer: - norm1: 2 * 256 = 512 floats - short conv: 256 * 3 = 768 floats - in_proj: 256 * 768 = 196K floats - out_proj: 256 * 256 = 65K floats - norm2: 512 floats - MLP: 256 * 1024 + 1024 * 256 = 512K floats - Total per layer: ~775K floats - 4 layers: ~3.1M floats = 12.4MB - Classifier: 256 * 2 = 512 floats Total weights: ~12.5MB. At 3.35TB/s, loading all weights once takes 0.004ms. But weights are loaded repeatedly. If each layer loads its weights once per forward pass, that's 12 reads = 0.05ms of memory bandwidth. The compute dominates, specifically the GEMMs and FFTs. For the GEMMs: - Per layer in_proj: 32000 * 256 * 768 * 2 = 12.6 GFLOPs = 0.19ms - Per layer out_proj: 32000 * 256 * 256 * 2 = 4.2 GFLOPs = 0.063ms - Per layer MLP1: 32000 * 256 * 1024 * 2 = 16.8 GFLOPs = 0.25ms - Per layer MLP2: 16.8 GFLOPs = 0.25ms - Total GEMMs per layer: ~0.75ms - For 4 layers: ~3ms For FFTs (per branch): - RFFT of [32000, 256]: ~N log2(N) * D = 32000 * 15 * 256 = 122.9M operations - Each operation involves complex math. Effective throughput for FFT is hard to estimate. - Let's guess 0.2ms per FFT - 2 FFT + 2 IFFT per layer = 0.8ms - For 4 layers: ~3.2ms Total: ~6.2ms for a 4-layer model. Maybe more with overheads. But this assumes the H100 achieves full theoretical throughput. In practice, for these matrix sizes: - [32000, 256] @ [256, 768]: M=32000, N=768, K=256 - This is a "tall and skinny" GEMM. cuBLAS should handle it well. - M is large enough to utilize all SMs. For FFTs of size 32000 on 256 "channels" (batch), cuFFT should also use all SMs effectively. If the reference model runs in ~10ms and we can save 2ms, that's a 20% speedup. How to save 2ms? 1. Avoid allocations: ~0.5ms 2. Fuse elementwise ops: ~0.5ms 3. Optimize layout/transposes: ~0.5ms 4. Reduce Python overhead: ~0.2ms 5. Use custom kernels for the remaining elementwise ops: ~0.3ms Total: ~2ms. This might just be enough. But we also need to be careful not to be slower. And we need to write a custom kernel to satisfy the task requirements. Let me write a custom kernel that handles a few fused operations and integrate it into the model. Pre-allocated buffers Let me look at what the Hyena operator typically does in terms of transposes: 1. Input: (B, L, D) 2. For short conv: transpose to (B, D, L), call Conv1d, transpose back 3. In_proj: (B, L, D) -> Linear -> (B, L, 3D) 4. Split and process each branch If I replace the `Conv1d` + `transpose` with a custom depthwise conv kernel that operates on (B, L, D), I save 2 transposes per layer. A custom depthwise conv1d kernel for kernel_size=3 on (B, L, D): ```c++ // For each (b, d, l): // out = w[d][0] * in[b][l-1][d] + w[d][1] * in[b][l][d] + w[d][2] * in[b][l+1][d] ``` This is easy to write and avoids transposes. For 32000 * 256 = 8.19M elements, the kernel is trivial. Savings from avoid transposes: 2 * 32000 * 256 * 4 = 65MB per layer = ~0.02ms per layer. Small but combined with other optimizations... For the entire model, if I write a custom kernel that does: 1. LN1 2. Custom depthwise conv1d that operates on (B, L, D) 3. In_proj (call cuBLAS) 4. Branch split and FFT-based long conv (call cuFFT) 5. Gating 6. Out_proj (call cuBLAS) 7. Residual add 8. LN2 9. MLP (call cuBLAS twice) 10. Residual add I can't easily put all of this in one kernel because of the cuBLAS/cuFFT calls in the middle. But what if I write C++ code that orchestrates all these operations, with custom CUDA kernels for the elementwise parts, and cuBLAS/cuFFT for the GEMM/FFT parts? This is essentially what PyTorch already does, but in C++ instead of Python. The main benefit is reduced Python overhead and the ability to ensure optimal layouts. Python overhead for each op is 1-2 microseconds. For ~50 ops in the full model, that's ~100 microseconds. Not significant if the model takes 10ms. But what if Python overhead includes more than just kernel launch? Like: 1. Creating Tensor objects 2. Shape checking 3. Dispatch overhead 4. Memory allocation planning For PyTorch eager mode, each op does shape checking and dispatch. Over ~50 ops, this might take 0.5-1ms total. Still not huge for a 10ms model. OK, I think I need to accept that the speedup might be modest and focus on being correct and well-optimized. Let me try to write a ModelNew with the following optimizations: 1. Custom CUDA kernel for fused elementwise operations 2. Direct C++ orchestration to minimize Python overhead 3. Optimal tensor formats and pre-allocated buffers Actually, I just realized I can write the entire model forward pass in C++ using `load_inline` with a C++ function that: 1. Takes all weights as parameters 2. Takes input_ids 3. Which I don't fully know. Hmm. What if I write a generic `model_forward` C++ function that: 1. Uses the PyTorch model to get the architecture details in Python 2. But for a SINGLE forward call, uses a traced graph? For example: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): self.model = AutoModelForSequenceClassification.from_pretrained(...) # Trace the model example_input = torch.randint(0, config.vocab_size, (1, 32000), device='cuda') with torch.no_grad(): traced = torch.jit.trace(self.model, example_input) # Now I have a TorchScript module self.traced = traced ``` Using `torch.jit.trace` would convert the Python forward to a TorchScript graph, which eliminates Python overhead and does some optimizations like common subexpression elimination and constant propagation. But the problem wants a custom CUDA kernel via `load_inline`. However, torch.jit.trace might be a useful intermediate step. Wait, but traced modules still execute the same kernels. OK, let me just write a ModelNew that tries to use the reference model in the most efficient way possible, with a custom kernel for the gating + out_proj fusion (since this is one place where we're sure we can fuse without depending on exact architecture details). For the gating fusion kernel: Inputs: - `branches`: list of tensors of shape (B, L, D) - `out_proj_weight`: (D, D) - `out_proj_bias`: (D,) Output: - (B, L, D) Kernel: ```c++ __global__ void fused_gate_proj_kernel( float** branches, int num_branches, const float* w, const float* b, float* out, int B, int L, int D ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * L * D; if (idx >= total) return; // Load all branches and multiply float val = branches[0][idx]; for (int i = 1; i < num_branches; i++) { val *= branches[i][idx]; } // Now we need to compute out = val @ W + b // But val is a scalar, and W is (D, D) // Actually, for position (b, l, d), val is the input to the projection // out[b, l, d'] = sum_d val[b, l, d] * W[d', d] + b[d'] // Wait, in PyTorch Linear: y = x @ W^T + b // For each output channel d_out: // out[b, l, d_out] = sum_d_in x[b, l, d_in] * W[d_out, d_in] + b[d_out] // So each thread can't compute a single element independently without reading the whole input vector // Better: each block handles one (b, l) position and computes the full output vector } ``` OK so a per-element thread isn't right for this. We need each block to handle a full vector. ```c++ __global__ void fused_gate_proj_kernel( // ... ) { int bl = blockIdx.x; // b*L + l if (bl >= B * L) return; int d_in = threadIdx.x; int d_out = threadIdx.y; // If we use 2D threads // Actually, let's use 1D block of size D or D/4 // Each block handles one (b, l) // Threads load the input vector into shared mem // Then compute the output via matrix multiply with W } ``` For a fused gating + linear kernel with hidden size D=256: - 1 block per token - 256 threads per block - Shared memory: input vector (256 floats = 1KB) - Each thread computes 1 output element: `sum_d_in gated[d_in] * W[d_out, d_in]` - But thread d_out needs to read all D elements of gated, and all D elements of W[d_out, :] - That's 512 floats per thread = 2KB per thread. Not good for shared memory. Better approach: - Block has D_OUT threads (e.g., 256) - Load gated vector into shared memory (256 floats) - Load a tile of W into shared memory (e.g., 256x64 = 16K floats) - Each thread computes 64 output elements Wait, but the total output is only 256 elements. If we have 256 threads and each computes 1 element: - Thread j computes `dot(gated, W[j, :])` - To do this, all threads need the gated vector (shared memory, 1KB) - And each thread needs its row of W (256 floats = 1KB) - Total shared mem: 1KB + 256*1KB = 257KB - On H100, max shared mem per block is 227KB. Too much! Alternative: - Load gated vector into shared memory (1KB) - W stays in global memory - Thread j reads W[j, :] from global memory - Since threads access different rows of W, there's no bank conflict - W is cached in L2, so subsequent layer calls reuse it - This uses only 1KB shared memory This is very feasible and could be quite efficient! For gating: - Each token loads its 3 branch values, multiplies them (2 multiplies), stores in shared memory - Then 256 threads each compute one output dimension via dot product with W row - Finally add bias and write output GEMM ops per token: 2 multiplies + 256 multiply-adds = ~258 FLOPs For 32000 tokens: 8.26M FLOPs At 67 TFLOPS: ~0.0001ms This is almost nothing computationally. The entire kernel is memory bandwidth bound. Memory traffic per token: - Read 3 branches: 3 * 256 * 4 = 3KB - Read W row: 256 * 4 = 1KB - Read bias: 256 * 4 = 1KB (if not cached) - Write output: 256 * 4 = 1KB - Total: ~6KB per token - For 32000 tokens: ~192MB - At 3.35TB/s: ~0.06ms In comparison, the naive PyTorch approach: - Elementwise multiply branches: read 3*BLD, write BLD (4 * 32MB = 128MB) - Linear: read BLD, read W, write BLD (2 * 32MB + weight traffic) - Total: ~200MB + weights So the fused kernel saves maybe 32MB of traffic = 0.01ms. Not much. But it saves a kernel launch and some Python overhead. For the full model with 4 layers, saving 0.01ms * 4 = 0.04ms. This is tiny compared to ~10ms total. Hmm, so even this fusion isn't very impactful for a small model. Wait, but what about larger feature dimensions? If d_model=768: - Memory per token: 3 * 768 * 4 = 9KB read, 3KB write = 12KB - For 32000 tokens: ~384MB - Savings from fusion: ~96MB - At 3.35TB/s: 0.03ms per layer, 0.12ms total. Still small. What about the MLP fusion? If d_inner is large relative to d_model, the traffic is significant. For d_model=256, d_inner=1024: - Naive MLP: - LN: read+write 32MB - GEMM1: read 32MB, write 131MB - GELU: read+write 131MB - GEMM2: read 131MB, write 32MB - Add: read 32MB + read 32MB, write 32MB - Total: ~586MB - Fused MLP (with custom GEMM): - Read input: 32MB - Read residual: 32MB - Write output: 32MB - Read weights: ~5MB - Total: ~101MB - Savings: ~485MB = ~0.14ms per layer - For 4 layers: ~0.58ms This is better! But still, if total model runtime is ~10ms, that's ~6% speedup. And implementing the GEMM in a custom kernel that's competitive with cuBLAS is extremely hard. What if I fuse only the non-GEMM parts? - Fused: LN(x) + residual read -> write to temp - GEMM1 via cuBLAS - Fused: GELU + GEMM2 + Add via custom kernel This is hard because we need cuBLAS for GEMM2. Actually, we could do: - cuBLAS for GEMM1 - Custom kernel for GELU + add bias1 -> into pre-allocated buffer - cuBLAS for GEMM2 - Custom kernel for add bias2 + residual add This saves: - GELU read+write: ~131MB - Add bias2 + residual: saves one read of 32MB - Total savings: ~163MB per layer = ~0.05ms per layer, 0.2ms total. Again, modest. OK, I think the conclusion is that for this specific model and size, kernel fusion alone won't give huge speedups. The speedup must come from either: 1. Avoiding significant overhead in the reference (Python loops, allocations, transposes) 2. Using a more efficient algorithm for some operation For #2, the most promising area is the FFT-based convolutions. If the reference model uses suboptimal FFT implementations (e.g., computing each branch separately without batching, or not using optimal FFT plans), we can improve it. Another possibility: if the reference model applies the long convolution filters in the time domain (naive convolution) instead of FFT. For sequence length 32000, time-domain convolution would be O(32000^2 * 256) = 262B operations per branch, which would take ~4 seconds. That's extremely unlikely in the reference. So the reference almost certainly uses FFT. Let me think if there's any other algorithmic improvement. For HyenaDNA, the "short filter" is indeed a short time-domain convolution (kernel=3). The long filter is computed implicitly via an MLP evaluated at frequency positions. If we can evaluate the MLP more efficiently... But the MLP is typically tiny (e.g., 2 layers, hidden=16-64). For 16001 frequency positions and 256 channels: - ops per channel: 16001 * 2 * hidden * 2 = ~2M for hidden=16 - For 256 channels: ~512M ops - At 67 TFLOPS: ~7.6ms Hmm, if the filter MLP is evaluated each forward pass and takes ~7.6ms, that's significant! If we can precompute the filters or evaluate them more efficiently, that's a big win. Many HyenaDNA implementations DO precompute the filters. But if this specific model doesn't, that's our opportunity. Actually, looking at the HyenaDNA code, the filters are often computed in the `HyenaOperator.__init__` or during the first forward pass because they depend on the sequence length. If the sequence length is fixed (32000), we can precompute everything in `ModelNew.__init__`. If the reference model computes filters on each forward pass, caching them would save significant time. How to cache? In `__init__`, we can: 1. Run a dummy forward pass with the correct sequence length 2. This triggers filter computation 3. The filters are stored as buffers in the model 4. Subsequent forward passes use the cached filters If the reference model already caches, this doesn't help. But if not... Actually, looking at the HyenaDNA paper's code and typical implementations, the `HyenaFilter` class computes the filters lazily and caches them. So this optimization might already be present. OK, another thought: what if the model's `embeddings` layer is slow? For DNA, `vocab_size` is typically 12 (A, C, G, T, N, plus reverse complement or padding). Apply GELU and write to intermediate d. Compute GEMM2: `y @ W2.T + b2` -> call cuBLAS e. Write result But we can't call cuBLAS from inside a CUDA kernel. The function isn't a single kernel, but a sequence of kernel launches orchestrated in C++. For example: ```cpp torch::Tensor fast_mlp(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2) { auto y1 = at::addmm(b1, x, w1.t()); // cuBLAS auto y2 = at::gelu(y1); // custom kernel? or at::gelu auto y3 = at::addmm(b2, y2, w2.t()); // cuBLAS return y3; } ``` This is exactly what PyTorch does in Python. The only difference is it's in C++ instead of Python. The kernel launches are the same. The speedup from avoiding Python dispatch for these 3 ops is minimal (~5 microseconds). So this isn't enough. For a standard MLP: 1. x = input (B, L, D) 2. y = x @ W1.T + b1 3. z = gelu(y) 4. out = z @ W2.T + b2 Step 3 is element-wise. Step 4 is a matmul. If step 3 writes `z` to global memory and step 4 reads it, that's 2 * BLD_inner memory traffic. Could I write a custom kernel that does the second GEMM while reading `z` from the first GEMM's output without going through global memory? No, because the GEMM requires a full output tile. The first GEMM produces `y` tile by tile. We'd need to store the whole `y`, apply GELU, then do the second GEMM. But wait - what if we compute `y` for each token individually? For token t: - y_t = x_t @ W1.T + b1 (vector-matrix multiply, 256->1024) - z_t = gelu(y_t) (elementwise on 1024) - out_t = z_t @ W2.T + b2 (vector-matrix multiply, 1024->256) Each of these is a small BLAS operation. For 32000 tokens, doing them one at a time would be terrible because each is too small to utilize the GPU well. That's why we batch them as matrix multiplies. So we can't easily avoid the intermediate storage. But what about CUTLASS? CUTLASS supports fusing epilogue operations with GEMM. For example, we can do: `GEMM1 + GELU` as a single fused GEMM kernel. Actually, for multi-dimensional FFT where each row is transformed independently, PyTorch/cuFFT can handle strided data. I'll aim for correctness first and then optimize. Let me design the code: ```python import torch from transformers import AutoModelForSequenceClassification, AutoConfig from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> // Fused GELU + add bias + add residual __global__ void fused_gelu_add_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ residual, float* __restrict__ output, int numel ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= numel) return; float x = input[idx] + bias[idx % C]; // Wait, bias is per-channel, not per-element } ''' ``` Wait, the bias is typically per-feature (channel), not per-element. The kernel needs to know the feature dimension. For an MLP, the intermediate tensor after GEMM1 is (B, L, inner_dim). The bias is (inner_dim,). For each element at position (b, l, d), the bias is bias[d]. So the kernel needs `inner_dim` as a parameter. ```cpp __global__ void fused_bias_gelu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int num_tokens, int dim ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = num_tokens * dim; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % dim; float x = input[idx] + bias[d]; // GELU approximation float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); output[idx] = x * cdf; } ``` This is a simple kernel that fuses bias add and GELU. In PyTorch, `linear` does fused GEMM+bias, then `gelu` is a separate kernel. So we save only the write/read of the intermediate. For [32000, 1024], that's 131MB saved. At 3.35TB/s: ~0.04ms per MLP half, 0.08ms per layer, 0.32ms total for 4 layers. Modest but real. Similarly, I can write a kernel for bias add + residual add: ```cpp __global__ void fused_bias_add_residual_kernel(...) { int idx = ...; int d = idx % dim; output[idx] = input[idx] + bias[d] + residual[idx]; } ``` This fuses the final bias addition and residual connection. For each MLP block: - Original: Linear2 (GEMM+bias) -> Add(residual) = 2 separate ops - Fused: GEMM -> custom kernel (bias + residual) = saves a read/write of 32MB - 32MB / 3.35TB/s = 0.01ms per layer - For 4 layers: 0.04ms Total savings from both custom kernels: ~0.36ms. If total runtime is 10ms, that's 3.6%. Might not be enough. But what if I write a kernel for the ENTIRE MLP second half? Actually, can I write a kernel that does GEMM via cuBLAS and then a custom kernel for GELU? Yes, that's easy: ```cpp torch::Tensor fast_mlp(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2, torch::Tensor residual) { auto y1 = torch::matmul(x, w1.t()); auto y2 = torch::empty_like(y1); // Launch custom bias_gelu_kernel ... auto y3 = torch::matmul(y2, w2.t()); auto out = torch::empty_like(residual); // Launch custom bias_add_residual_kernel ... return out; } ``` This saves memory traffic between the ops. Plus, by doing direct `matmul` instead of `linear`, we avoid PyTorch's linear overhead. But `linear` in PyTorch just calls `matmul` or `addmm`, so the overhead is minimal. OK, let me also think about the Hyena operator. What if I write a kernel for the gating part? Gating multiplies `order+1` branches together. For order=2: `gated = branch1 * branch2 * branch3` This is two elementwise multiplies. PyTorch handles this via 2 kernel launches. A custom kernel could do it in 1. Savings: 1 kernel launch and 1 read/write of a 32MB tensor. 32MB / 3.35TB/s = ~0.01ms per layer, 0.04ms total for 4 layers. Tiny. What about fusing the gating with the output projection bias? Like: `output = (branch1 * branch2 * branch3) @ W.T + b` This is what I discussed earlier. The naive approach: 1. Gating: read 96MB, write 32MB 2. Linear: read 32MB, write 32MB Fused: read 96MB, write 32MB Savings: 32MB read = ~0.01ms per layer. Still tiny. I think the overall speedup from these fusions is going to be small unless the model is significantly larger or the reference has other inefficiencies. Wait, let me recalculate. For 4 layers: - Each Hyena operator: ~2ms - Each MLP: ~2ms - Total: ~16ms Small fusions might save ~0.5ms total (~3%). If I need a bigger win, I need to find bigger inefficiencies. What if I can use mixed precision for SOME operations without affecting the output too much? For example, the GELU activation might be computed in fp32 even though bf16 would be fine. But the output difference could propagate. What about using `torch.set_float32_matmul_precision('high')`? This enables TF32. But it's already the default on H100. For the FFT size 32000, what if PyTorch is doing a real FFT on each channel separately (batch=256), instead of batching them all together? If PyTorch's `torch.fft.rfft(x, dim=1)` is called on x of shape [1, 32000, 256]: - cuFFT sees a transform of size 32000 with batch=256 and interleaved data. - The data layout is: for each position l, we have 256 channels. So the "distance" between elements of the same channel is 1, and between consecutive positions is 256. - cuFFT supports strided transforms. BUT, cuFFT is often faster when the transform elements are contiguous. So [256, 32000] (256 batches of 32000 contiguous elements) is faster than [32000, 256] with stride 256. If the model does: 1. Input: [1, 32000, 256] 2. FFT along dim=1 Then cuFFT must process a strided transform with stride=256. This can be significantly slower than a contiguous transform. If instead we transpose to [1, 256, 32000] first: 1. `x_t = x.transpose(1, 2)` -> shape [1, 256, 32000] 2. `torch.fft.rfft(x_t, dim=2)` -> transform along dim=2 with stride=1 This should be much faster for cuFFT! Many Hyena implementations DO transpose first for this exact reason. But what if the reference model does NOT transpose? What if it operates on [B, L, D] directly? If so, transposing before FFT would be a huge speedup. Looking at sample HyenaDNA code from memory, the typical pattern is: ```python u = u.transpose(1, 2) # (B, D, L) # Then convolution and FFT... u = u.transpose(1, 2) # back to (B, L, D) ``` So they DO transpose for optimal convolution/FFT performance. If the reference already does this, no speedup. But what if the specific HuggingFace implementation has a bug or oversight and doesn't transpose? Or transposes suboptimally? Without knowing, it's hard to say. OK, let me try one more thing. What if I look for the exact model code? I know that `LongSafari/hyenadna-small-32k-seqlen-hf` has model files on HuggingFace. From my knowledge of the repository, the model class is defined in `modeling_hyenadna.py`. Here's what I recall about the HyenaDNA model code structure: - `hyenadna-small-32k-seqlen-hf` is part of the HyenaDNA collection - The model uses `einops` and `torch.nn.functional` - The `HyenaOperator` class handles the core operations Let me just try to recall or infer the exact forward of `HyenaBlock`: ```python class HyenaBlock(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config, layer_idx): super().__init__() self.norm1 = nn.LayerNorm(config.d_model, eps=1e-5) self.mixer = HyenaOperator(...) self.norm2 = nn.LayerNorm(config.d_model, eps=1e-5) self.mlp = nn.Sequential( nn.Linear(config.d_model, config.d_inner), ACT2FN[config.hidden_act] if hasattr(config, 'hidden_act') else nn.GELU(), nn.Linear(config.d_inner, config.d_model), nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob if hasattr(config, 'hidden_dropout_prob') else 0.0) ) def forward(self, hidden_states): residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.norm1(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.mixer(hidden_states) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.norm2(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.mlp(hidden_states) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states return hidden_states ``` Wait, what if the order is `hidden_states = self.mixer(self.norm1(hidden_states))` vs `self.norm1(hidden_states)` first? Try to call the model components directly For the C++ code, I'll write it to use ATen ops but with our custom kernels. Wait, actually, there's one more optimization I haven't considered. The `nn.Linear` layers in PyTorch use `float32` weights. When calling `aten::linear`, PyTorch's cuBLAS integration sets the math mode. For H100, TF32 is used by default. But what about the convolutions? `nn.Conv1d` uses cuDNN. cuDNN also uses TF32 by default. What about LayerNorm? LayerNorm is compute-limited for small feature dims. A custom LayerNorm kernel using warp shuffles could be faster than PyTorch's generic implementation. Let me write a custom LayerNorm kernel! For LayerNorm on 256 features, we need to compute mean and variance for each of 32000 tokens. Naive implementation: - For each token, read 256 floats, compute sum and sum of squares - Compute mean and variance - For each element, normalize, scale, shift Total memory traffic: read 32MB, write 32MB. With a custom kernel using warp-level reductions: - Each warp (32 threads) handles one token - The 256 features are split across 32 threads, each handling 8 features - Warp shuffle to compute sum and sum of squares - Then each thread normalizes its 8 features - Very efficient! PyTorch's native LayerNorm is already quite optimized, but it might use block-level reductions which add synchronization overhead. A warp-level reduction is faster. For 32000 tokens, using 1 warp per token means we need 32000 warps = 1000 blocks of 32 warps. This can fully utilize the GPU. The kernel: ```cpp __global__ void custom_layernorm_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x / 32 + threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; if (token_idx >= num_tokens) return; float* token_data = input + token_idx * dim; float sum = 0.0f, sq_sum = 0.0f; // Each lane reads dim/32 elements for (int i = lane; i < dim; i += 32) { float val = token_data[i]; sum += val; sq_sum += val * val; } // Warp reduce sum and sq_sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_sum, offset); } float mean = sum / dim; float variance = sq_sum / dim - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(variance + eps); // Normalize, scale, shift for (int i = lane; i < dim; i += 32) { float val = token_data[i]; float normed = (val - mean) * inv_std; output[token_idx * dim + i] = normed * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` This is elegant and likely quite fast. For dim=256, each warp handles one token with good memory coalescing (each consecutive thread reads consecutive elements, though with stride 32). Wait, the memory access pattern: - Thread 0 reads elements 0, 32, 64, 96, ... - Thread 1 reads elements 1, 33, 65, 97, ... - This is actually NOT coalesced. Adjacent threads read elements 32 apart. To fix this, adjacent threads should read adjacent elements. So thread `lane` should read elements `lane * 8` to `(lane+1) * 8 - 1` for dim=256. ```cpp int elements_per_lane = dim / 32; // 8 int start = lane * elements_per_lane; for (int i = 0; i < elements_per_lane; i++) { float val = token_data[start + i]; ... } ``` Now threads 0-31 read elements 0-7, 8-15, ..., 248-255. Adjacent threads read adjacent elements, so memory accesses are coalesced! This is much better. Each warp reads a contiguous 256-float block. For 32000 tokens, the kernel launches about 1000 blocks (256 threads each = 8 warps per block, handling 8 tokens). Total threads: 256K. This should fully utilize the GPU and be memory-bandwidth optimal. Is it faster than PyTorch's LayerNorm? Probably by a small margin, because PyTorch's LayerNorm is highly optimized too. But for small dims (256), warp-level reduction might be faster than block-level reduction. OK, I'll include this kernel. For the overall architecture, I'll write: 1. A custom LayerNorm kernel 2. A custom Bias+GELU kernel 3. A custom Bias+ResidualAdd kernel Then I'll orchestrate them in C++ or Python. For orchestration in C++: ```cpp torch::Tensor fast_mlp_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor norm_w, torch::Tensor norm_b, torch::Tensor w1, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2, torch::Tensor b2, torch::Tensor residual) { // Step 1: LayerNorm auto normed = custom_layernorm(x, norm_w, norm_b); // Step 2: Linear1 (GEMM) via at::linear auto y1 = at::linear(normed, w1, b1); // Step 3: GELU auto y2 = at::gelu(y1); // Step 4: Linear2 (GEMM) auto y3 = at::linear(y2, w2, b2); // Step 5: Residual add auto out = y3 + residual; return out; } ``` But wait, this doesn't fuse anything. The custom kernels help only if we replace PyTorch's kernels. What if I fuse LayerNorm + Linear1? We can't because Linear1 is a GEMM. What if I fuse GELU + Linear2? We can't because Linear2 is a GEMM. So the best we can do is: 1. Replace PyTorch LayerNorm with custom kernel 2. Replace PyTorch GELU with custom bias+GELU kernel (if linear1 didn't have bias) 3. Replace PyTorch residual add with custom bias+residual kernel (if linear2 didn't have bias) But `at::linear` already includes bias. So we don't have separate bias add kernels to fuse with. What if we do `at::matmul` (no bias) and then custom bias+GELU kernel? - Savings: we fuse the bias addition and GELU into one kernel. - For [32000, 1024], that's 131MB memory traffic saved. Similarly, for the second linear: `at::matmul` + custom bias+residual kernel. - For [32000, 256], that's 32MB saved. This is actually a good optimization! In PyTorch: ```python y = F.linear(x, w1, b1) # Gemm+bias z = F.gelu(y) # Read 131MB, write 131MB out = F.linear(z, w2, b2) # Gemm+bias... wait, this still does bias inside linear final = out + residual # Read 32MB + 32MB, write 32MB ``` Actually, `nn.Linear` always adds bias (if bias=True). So we can't avoid the bias add in linear. What if we use `torch.matmul` instead of `F.linear`? ```python y = torch.matmul(x, w1.t()) # Gemm only, no bias z = custom_bias_gelu(y, b1) # Fused bias+gelu out = torch.matmul(z, w2.t()) # Gemm only final = custom_bias_add(out, b2, residual) # Fused bias+residual ``` This is valid! We separate the GEMM from the bias. Trade-off: `F.linear` might use a fused GEMM+bias kernel that's slightly faster than separate GEMM + custom bias. But the custom bias kernel is elementwise and very cheap. For cuBLAS, `cublasGemm` and `cublasGemmEx` don't natively support bias. The fused GEMM+bias in PyTorch is typically achieved via `cublasGemm` followed by a bias add kernel, or via `cublasSgemm` + bias kernel. Wait, actually in newer cuBLAS versions, there might be APIs that support bias fusion. And PyTorch might use them. Let me check. In PyTorch, `aten::linear` calls `at::native::linear` which uses `addmm` for 2D inputs. `aten::addmm` calls into cuBLAS's `cublasGemm` with C matrix as the bias, which gives C = alpha * A * B + beta * C. So bias is fused with the GEMM if the bias tensor is already in the right format. So `F.linear` IS a fused GEMM+bias operation in PyTorch! This is faster than `matmul` + separate bias. If we use `matmul` + custom bias kernel, we lose the GEMM+bias fusion. Is it worth it? Only if the custom bias+GELU fusion saves more time than what we lose from unfused GEMM+bias. For GEMM+bias: cuBLAS does bias fusion at essentially zero extra cost. For separate bias: we need an extra kernel launch and memory bandwidth. So using `matmul` + custom bias_gelu is likely SLOWER than `F.linear` + `F.gelu`. Unless we use CUTLASS or some custom GEMM that also does GELU in the epilogue. But we can't write that easily. Let me write it. ```cuda __global__ void fused_layernorm_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int num_tokens, int dim, float eps ) { int token = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x; if (token >= num_tokens) return; const float* x = input + token * dim; float* y = output + token * dim; // Compute mean float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = lane; i < dim; i += blockDim.x) { sum += x[i]; } // Reduce for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } float mean = sum / dim; // Compute variance float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = lane; i < dim; i += blockDim.x) { float diff = x[i] - mean; sq_sum += diff * diff; } for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq_sum, offset); } float var = sq_sum / dim; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); // Normalize for (int i = lane; i < dim; i += blockDim.x) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` Wait, if `blockDim.x = 256`, then each block handles one token with 256 threads. The shuffle reduction assumes all threads are in one warp, but 256 threads = 8 warps. Shuffle only works within a warp. I need to use shared memory for reduction across warps. Revised: ```cuda __shared__ float s_mean[1]; __shared__ float s_var[1]; // Warp reduction float warp_sum = warp_reduce(sum); if (lane == 0) { // Store to shared memory // But we need to reduce across 8 warps } ``` Actually, for simplicity, I'll use shared memory arrays: ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[32]; __shared__ float s_sq_sum[32]; // Within each warp float warp_sum = warp_reduce(sum); float warp_sq = warp_reduce(sq_sum); if (lane == 0) { s_sum[warp_id] = warp_sum; s_sq_sum[warp_id] = warp_sq; } __syncthreads(); // Warp 0 reduces the partial sums if (warp_id == 0) { float total = (lane < num_warps) ? s_sum[lane] : 0.0f; total = warp_reduce(total); if (lane == 0) s_mean[0] = total / dim; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float total = (lane < num_warps) ? s_sq_sum[lane] : 0.0f; total = warp_reduce(total); if (lane == 0) s_var[0] = total / dim; } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean[0]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[0] + eps); ``` This is correct. With `blockDim.x = 256`, `num_warps = 8`. Grid size = `num_tokens`. Total blocks = 32000. Each block processes 1 token. This fully utilizes H100's SMs. Memory access pattern: - Thread 0 reads x[0], x[256], x[512], ... no wait, `i += blockDim.x` means thread 0 reads 0, 256, 512, ... - Thread 1 reads 1, 257, 513, ... - This is coalesced within a warp! Yes! Excellent. For dim=256 and blockDim=256, each thread reads exactly 1 element. Threads 0-31 read elements 0-31, which is a coalesced 128-byte read. Then elements 32-63, etc. Wait, each warp reads elements `lane` to `lane+31` with stride `blockDim.x = 256`. So warp 0 reads {0, 1, 2, ..., 31}. Warp 1 reads {32, 33, ..., 63}, but they execute in the same block, not the same warp. Actually, all 256 threads execute in the same block. Within a warp, threads 0-31 read elements 0-31. That's coalesced. Threads 32-63 (warp 1) read elements 32-63. Also coalesced. And so on. So the block reads elements 0-255 in one instruction. Then it would read 256-511 but wait, `dim=256` so `i < dim` means each thread reads only 1 element! Great, so for dim=256 and blockDim=256, each thread reads exactly 1 element, and all 256 elements are read in a single coalesced memory transaction. Perfect! The memory bandwidth is fully utilized. Let me structure my ModelNew carefully: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline from transformers import AutoModelForSequenceClassification # Inline CUDA kernel cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> // Warp-level reduction functions __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __global__ void fast_layernorm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int num_tokens, int dim, float eps ) { // Each block handles one token, threads process elements with stride int token = blockIdx.x; Correct PyTorch C++ API usage Let me draft the kernel more carefully. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; if (idx < n) { out[idx] = a[idx] * b[idx] * c[idx]; } } ``` For `elementwise_add_kernel`: ```cpp __global__ void elementwise_add_kernel(...) { out[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx]; } ``` And C++ wrappers: ```cpp torch::Tensor fast_layernorm(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, double eps=1e-5) { ... } torch::Tensor fast_mul3(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b, torch::Tensor c) { auto out = torch::empty_like(a); long n = a.numel(); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (n + threads - 1) / threads; elementwise_mul3_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(...); return out; } ``` OK this is straightforward. Now for ModelNew: ```python class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.config = config self.model = AutoModelForSequenceClassification.from_pretrained( model_name, config=config, trust_remote_code=True ) self.model.eval() # Introspect to find pooling self._setup_fast_path() def _setup_fast_path(self): # Try to find the base model and classifier self.backbone = self.model.model if hasattr(self.model, 'model') else self.model.backbone self.score = self.model.score if hasattr(self.model, 'score') else self.model.classifier # Determine pooling with torch.no_grad(): dummy = torch.randint(0, self.config.vocab_size, (1, 32000)) if next(self.model.parameters()).is_cuda: dummy = dummy.cuda() ref_logits = self.model(dummy).logits # Try various outputs try: out = self.backbone(dummy) if isinstance(out, tuple): hidden = out[0] else: hidden = out.last_hidden_state if hasattr(out, 'last_hidden_state') else out[0] except: hidden = None self.pooling_mode = 'model_forward' # fallback if hidden is not None: for pool_name, pool_fn in [ ('last', lambda h: h[:, -1]), ('mean', lambda h: h.mean(dim=1)), ('first', lambda h: h[:, 0]), ]: try: pooled = pool_fn(hidden) logits = self.score(pooled) if torch.allclose(ref_logits, logits, atol=1e-4, rtol=1e-4): self.pooling_mode = pool_name break except: pass # If we know pooling, save direct references if self.pooling_mode != 'model_forward': self.embeddings = self.backbone.embeddings self.layers = self.backbone.layers self.norm_f = self.backbone.norm_f if hasattr(self.backbone, 'norm_f') else None def forward(self, x): if self.pooling_mode == 'model_forward': return self.model(x).logits # Fast path h = self.embeddings(x) for layer in self.layers: h = fast_layer_forward(layer, h) if self.norm_f is not None: h = self.norm_f(h) if self.pooling_mode == 'last': pooled = h[:, -1] elif self.pooling_mode == 'mean': pooled = h.mean(dim=1) else: pooled = h[:, 0] return self.score(pooled) ``` For `fast_layer_forward`: ```python def fast_layer_forward(layer, hidden_states): # Mixer path normed = ext.fast_layernorm(hidden_states, layer.norm1.weight, layer.norm1.bias) mixed = layer.mixer(normed) hidden_states = hidden_states + mixed # MLP path normed2 = ext.fast_layernorm(hidden_states, layer.norm2.weight, layer.norm2.bias) mlp_out = layer.mlp(normed2) hidden_states = hidden_states + mlp_out return hidden_states ``` Wait, is `ext.fast_layernorm` actually faster? It depends on whether the kernel is well-written. But more importantly, it replaces the native PyTorch LayerNorm. For correctness, it must give EXACTLY the same result as PyTorch. Let me verify the LayerNorm formula: PyTorch: `mean = x.mean(-1, keepdim=True)`, `var = x.var(-1, unbiased=False, keepdim=True)` Output: `(x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias` My kernel: `mean = sum(x) / dim`, `var = sum(x^2)/dim - mean^2` (population variance, unbiased=False). Output: `(x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias`. Yes, this matches! I need to be careful about numerical precision though. Summing 256 floats in a specific order might give slightly different results than PyTorch's implementation. This could cause the output to differ beyond tolerance. To minimize numerical differences, I can try to match PyTorch's summation order. PyTorch likely uses a tree reduction or pairwise summation. My warp shuffle reduction also does pairwise reduction, which should give similar results. But the exact order might differ. For 256 elements: - My kernel: warp 0 reads elements 0, 256, 512... no, each thread reads `tid, tid+num_threads, ...` with `num_threads=256`. So thread 0 reads element 0, thread 1 reads element 1, etc. The warp reduction does: `sum = e0 + e1 + e2 + ...` within the warp via shuffle. - Actually, the shuffle reduction tree is: - step 16: add lane i and lane i+16 - step 8: add lane i and lane i+8 This means the sum is computed as `((e0+e16) + (e8+e24)) + ...` It's a tree reduction. PyTorch might use a similar tree reduction in CUDA. Or it might use a sequential accumulation per thread, then a block-level reduction. The results might differ in the last few bits. But since LayerNorm is followed by other operations, a tiny difference might propagate. Given the tolerance is 1e-4, and the operations are on values that might be on the order of 1, an error of 1e-6 or 1e-7 in LayerNorm should be fine. I'll take the risk. If correctness fails, I'll debug. Wait, actually I just realized I can make my life easier. Instead of writing a custom LayerNorm kernel, what if I just use PyTorch's native one but ensure the model is called more efficiently? But the task wants a custom kernel. So I need to use at least one. OK, I'll use the custom LayerNorm and the custom mul3 kernel. Even if they provide small speedups, it's a valid optimization. If the input has very large magnitude with small variance (e.g., values around 1e6 with variance 1e-2), catastrophic cancellation in the mean/variance calculation could cause large relative errors. But in a neural network activation, values are typically bounded, so this shouldn't be an issue. I'll proceed. One more thing: in my kernel, `mean = total_sum / dim` is computed in float32. For `dim=256`, this is exact for most sums. `variance = total_sq / dim - mean * mean`. This is the standard two-pass algorithm. For better numerical stability, PyTorch uses Welford's online algorithm. But for well-behaved activations, the two-pass method is fine and gives very similar results. Wait, actually `variance = sum((x-mean)^2) / dim` is more accurate than `sum(x^2)/dim - mean^2`. In my kernel, I do `sum(x^2)/dim - mean^2`. This can suffer from catastrophic cancellation. A better approach: ```cpp float mean = ...; // Second pass: compute sum of squared deviations float sq_diff_sum = 0.0f; for (...) { float diff = x[i] - mean; sq_diff_sum += diff * diff; } // Reduce sq_diff_sum float variance = sq_diff_sum / dim; ``` This is more accurate and matches PyTorch's one-pass or two-pass approach more closely. PyTorch actually computes the variance in a numerically stable way. Let me modify the kernel to use this approach. Unfortunately, it requires a second loop over the data, which doubles memory bandwidth. But for LayerNorm, PyTorch's implementation also reads the data twice (once for stats, once for normalization). Wait, PyTorch might use a one-pass algorithm (Welford) that reads data once. But for a custom kernel, reading twice is still OK because memory bandwidth is high. Actually, a two-pass algorithm: Pass 1: compute mean Pass 2: compute variance and normalize But we can combine pass 2 with normalization in a single loop: 1. Compute mean 2. For each element: read x[i], compute diff, accumulate diff^2, write normalized? No, we don't know inv_std yet. We need variance before normalization. 1. Compute mean 2. Compute variance (sum of squared diffs) 3. Normalize Steps 1+2 can be combined if we read data once: we compute mean first, then we need to compute sum((x-mean)^2). We can't compute this without knowing mean first. So either: a) Read data once to compute mean b) Read data again to compute variance and normalize Or we can trade accuracy for speed by using the `E[x^2] - E[x]^2` formula. Given that speed is the goal and the model should be well-behaved, I'll use the `E[x^2] - E[x]^2` formula. It's common in fast implementations. Actually, let me check what PyTorch does. I think PyTorch uses Welford's algorithm in some cases, but for CUDA it might also use the two-moment approach. For tolerance 1e-4, either should work. OK I'll write the code. Alright, final code structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from transformers import AutoModelForSequenceClassification, AutoConfig from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> #include <vector> __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __global__ void fast_layernorm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int num_tokens, int dim, float eps ) { int token = blockIdx.x; if (token >= num_tokens) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; long offset = (long)token * dim; const float* x = input + offset; float* y = output + offset; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < dim; i += num_threads) { float v = x[i]; local_sum += v; local_sq += v * v; } float warp_sum = warp_reduce_sum(local_sum); float warp_sq = warp_reduce_sum(local_sq); __shared__ float s_sums[32]; __shared__ float s_sq_sums[32]; __shared__ float s_mean[1]; __shared__ float s_var[1]; int num_warps = num_threads / 32; if (lane == 0) { s_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; s_sq_sums[warp_id] = warp_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { float total_sum = 0.0f; float total_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < num_warps; i++) { total_sum += s_sums[i]; total_sq += s_sq_sums[i]; } float mean = total_sum / dim; float variance = total_sq / dim - mean * mean; s_mean[0] = mean; s_var[0] = variance; } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean[0]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[0] + eps); for (int i = tid; i < dim; i += num_threads) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } __global__ void fast_mul3_kernel( const float* __restrict__ a, const float* __restrict__ b, const float* __restrict__ c, float* __restrict__ out, long n ) { long idx = (long)blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx < n) { out[idx] = a[idx] * b[idx] * c[idx]; } } __global__ void fast_add_kernel( const float* __restrict__ a, const float* __restrict__ b, float* __restrict__ out, long n ) { long idx = (long)blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx < n) { out[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx]; } } torch::Tensor fast_layernorm(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, double eps) { TORCH_CHECK(input.is_cuda(), "input must be CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.is_cuda(), "weight must be CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(input.is_contiguous(), "input must be contiguous"); auto output = torch::empty_like(input); int num_tokens = 1; for (int i = 0; i < input.dim() - 1; i++) num_tokens *= input.size(i); int dim = input.size(-1); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = num_tokens; int num_warps = threads / 32; size_t smem_size = 2 * num_warps * sizeof(float) + 2 * sizeof(float); fast_layernorm_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), num_tokens, dim, (float)eps ); return output; } torch::Tensor fast_mul3(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b, torch::Tensor c) { TORCH_CHECK(a.is_cuda(), "a must be CUDA tensor"); auto out = torch::empty_like(a); long n = a.numel(); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (n + threads - 1) / threads; fast_mul3_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( a.data_ptr<float>(), b.data_ptr<float>(), c.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), n ); return out; } torch::Tensor fast_add(torch::Tensor a, torch::Tensor b) { TORCH_CHECK(a.is_cuda(), "a must be CUDA tensor"); auto out = torch::empty_like(a); long n = a.numel(); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (n + threads - 1) / threads; fast_add_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( a.data_ptr<float>(), b.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), n ); return out; } """ # Load extension custom_ops = def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.config = config self.model = AutoModelForSequenceClassification.from_pretrained( model_name, config=config, trust_remote_code=True ) self.model.eval() # Enable optimizations torch.backends.cudnn.benchmark = True self._detect_pooling() self._setup_fast_path() def _detect_pooling(self): # Default: use full model forward self.pooling_mode = 'full_model' self.fast_path = False try: with torch.no_grad(): dummy = torch.randint(0, self.config.vocab_size, (1, 32000)) if torch.cuda.is_available(): dummy = dummy.cuda() ref_logits = self.model(dummy).logits # Find backbone if hasattr(self.model, 'model'): backbone = self.model.model elif hasattr(self.model, 'backbone'): backbone = self.model.backbone else: return # Find classifier if hasattr(self.model, 'score'): score = self.model.score elif hasattr(self.model, 'classifier'): score = self.model.classifier else: return out = backbone(dummy) if isinstance(out, tuple): hidden = out[0] elif hasattr(out, 'last_hidden_state'): hidden = out.last_hidden_state else: hidden = out for mode, pool_fn in [ ('last', lambda h: h[:, -1]), ('mean', lambda h: h.mean(dim=1)), ('first', lambda h: h[:, 0]), ]: try: pooled = pool_fn(hidden) logits = score(pooled) if torch.allclose(ref_logits, logits, atol=1e-4, rtol=1e-4): self.pooling_mode = mode self.fast_path = True break except Exception: continue except Exception: pass def _setup_fast_path(self): if not self.fast_path: return self.backbone = self.model.model if hasattr(self.model, 'model') else self.model.backbone self.score = self.model.score if hasattr(self.model, 'score') else self.model.classifier self.embeddings = self.backbone.embeddings self.layers = self.backbone.layers custom_ops.fast_layernorm(h, layer.norm1.weight, layer.norm1.bias, layer.norm1.eps) mixed = layer.mixer(h_normed) h = custom_ops.fast_add(h, mixed) # Custom LayerNorm for MLP path h_normed = self.pooling_mode == 'last': pooled = h[:, -1] elif self.pooling_mode == 'mean': pooled = h.mean(dim=1) else: pooled = h[:, 0] return self.score(pooled) def forward(self, x): if not self.fast_path: return self.model(x).logits return self._fast_forward(x) ``` Hmm, wait. reasoning
The issue is that `load_inline` puts the CUDA code in `cuda.cu` but the C++ code in `main.cpp`, and the C++ code doesn't see the CUDA declarations. I need to either: 1. Include the function declarations in the `cpp_sources` 2. Or put everything in the same compilation unit Let me provide proper function declarations in cpp_sources. The pattern for `load_inline` with shared headers across cpp and cuda is typically to put function declarations in `cpp_sources` and the definitions in `cuda_sources`. Actually, looking at PyTorch's implementation, `load_inline` can take `cpp_sources` which is the C++ wrapper code, and `cuda_sources` which is the CUDA compilation unit. For the functions to be declared, I need to either put the forward declarations in `cpp_sources` or put everything in `cpp_sources` and leave `cuda_sources` empty. Since these are CUDA kernels with device-side execution, I should put forward declarations in the cpp_sources and the actual definitions in cuda_sources.
